	=>  long count = list.stream().count();
        System.out.println(count);
        List<Employee> sorted = list.stream().sorted(Comparator.comparing(Employee::getSalary).reversed().thenComparing(Employee::getName)).toList();
		sorted.forEach(n-> System.out.println(n.getName()));
		
122. which design pattern have actually implemeneted in context of microservices ?

		1. API Gateway Pattern
			≡ÓÈí Purpose :
				Provides a single entry point for all clients
				Handles routing, authentication, load balancing, and protocol translation

	≡Óõ╣ Implementation :
			=> Use Spring Cloud Gateway or Netflix Zuul

				Example:
				Client ΓÁÈ API Gateway ΓÁÈ Service A
									 ΓÁÈ Service B

				Features: rate limiting, request aggregation, authentication, logging
				
		2. Circuit Breaker Pattern :
			≡ÓÈí Purpose
				Prevents cascading failures
				Fails fast when a downstream service is unavailable

		≡Óõ╣ Implementation
			=> Use Resilience4j or Spring Cloud Circuit Breaker
				Example:

					@CircuitBreaker(name = "employeeService", fallbackMethod = "fallbackEmployees")
					public List<Employee> getEmployees() {
						return restTemplate.getForObject("http://employee-service/employees", List.class);
					}

					public List<Employee> fallbackEmployees(Throwable t) {
						return List.of(); // return empty list as fallback
					}
					
		3. Service Discovery Pattern :
				≡ÓÈí Purpose
					Services register themselves dynamically
					Clients discover services without hardcoding URLs

		≡Óõ╣ Implementation
			=> Use Eureka, Consul, or Zookeeper
				Example:
					Employee Service registers with Eureka
					Payroll Service queries Eureka to find Employee Service
	
		4. Saga Pattern / Distributed Transaction
				≡ÓÈí Purpose
					Ensure data consistency across multiple microservices
					Avoid distributed 2PC (slow & brittle)

		≡Óõ╣ Implementation :
				Orchestration-based Sagas: Spring Boot + Camunda/Zeebe
				Choreography-based Sagas: Kafka / Event-driven approach
				
123. ≡Óõã Sync vs Async (Synchronous vs Asynchronous) :

	=> In a synchronous call, the client sends a request and waits for the response before continuing.
			Example:
			A standard REST API call:
				Service A ΓÁÈ calls Service B
				Service A waits
				Service B responds
				Service A continues
			Characteristics:
				Blocking
				Immediate response required
				Simple requestΓÇôresponse pattern
				Tight coupling (Service A depends on Service B being available)
				
	=> In an asynchronous model, the client sends a message and does not wait for an immediate response.
			Example:
			Using a message broker like Apache Kafka or RabbitMQ:
				Service A publishes a message
				Service A continues immediately
				Service B processes the message later
			Characteristics:
				Non-blocking
				Event-driven
				Loose coupling
				Better resilience
				
	==> when to use Kafka/RabbitMQ(Async) :
		1. Decoupling services:
			Services should not fail just because another service is down.
			Example:
				Order Service publishes "OrderCreated"
				Inventory, Email, Billing consume it independently.
				
		2.High throughput / scalability
			Apache Kafka is especially good for:
				Millions of events
				Event streaming
				Real-time analytics
				Log aggregation
				
124. how do differ from @RestController, @Service, @Repository differ in terms of Application context ?
	=> All three:
		@RestController
		@Service
		@Repository
			=> Are Spring-managed components.
			=> they are detected during the component scanning and registred as beans in application context.
			
	1. @RestController : @Controller + @ResponseBody
			≡ÓôÔ Purpose
					Used in the web layer to handle HTTP requests.
					
			≡Óôª What happens in ApplicationContext?
					Registered as a bean
					Registered with Spring MVCΓÇÕs HandlerMapping
					Methods are mapped to HTTP endpoints
					Responses are automatically serialized (JSON/XML)
	2. @Service
			@Service is a specialization of @Component
			≡ÓôÔ Purpose
				Represents business logic layer.
				
			≡Óôª What happens in ApplicationContext?
				Registered as a bean
				No additional behavior by default
				Used for semantic clarity
			
	3. @Repository
			Also a specialization of @Component
			=> But this one has extra behavior.

			≡ÓôÔ Purpose
				Represents data access layer.
			≡Óôª What happens in ApplicationContext?
				Registered as a bean
				Automatically participates in PersistenceExceptionTranslation


125.  what are the components of Kafka ?
	≡Óõ╣ Core Kafka Components :
	
	1. Producer :
		Publishes (writes) records to Kafka topics
		Responsible for:
			Choosing topic & partition
			Serialization
			Retries & acknowledgements.
			
	2. Consumer :
		Subscribes to topics and reads records
			Works in consumer groups
		Responsible for:
			Offset management
			Message processing
			
	3. Topic :
		A logical stream of data
		Identified by a name
		Data is written and read only via topics
		
	4. Partition :
			Topics are split into partitions
		Enables:
			Parallelism
			Scalability
			Each partition is an ordered, immutable log
			
	5. Broker
		Kafka server that:
			Stores data
			Serves producers & consumers
		=> A Kafka cluster has multiple brokers.
		
	6. Kafka Cluster :
		=> A group of brokers working together
		Provides:
			Load balancing
			Fault tolerance
			High availability
			
	7. Offset :
		A unique sequence number per partition
		Identifies a consumerΓÇÕs position in the log
		
	8. Consumer Group :
		=> Logical group of consumers
		Kafka ensures:
			One partition ΓÁÈ one consumer in a group
			Enables horizontal scaling
			
	9. Replication :
		Each partition has replicas
			Leader replica
			Follower replicas
		=> Ensures fault tolerance
		
	10. Leader & Follower :
			Leader: Handles all reads/writes
			Followers: Replicate leaderΓÇÕs data
	
		
126. ≡Óõ╣ What are equals() and hashCode()?

	=> Both methods come from java.lang.Object and are heavily used by hash-based collections like HashMap, HashSet, Hashtable.
	
	| Method       | Purpose                                                         | return type |
	| ------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------- |	---------- |
	| `equals()`   | Checks **logical equality** (content comparison)                |	boolean     |
	| `hashCode()` | Helps Java **quickly locate objects** in hash-based collections |	int         |
	
	≡ÓÀÊ Rule:
	If two objects are equal according to equals(), they must have the same hashCode().
	
	=> ≡Óõ╣ How hashCode() works INTERNALLY ≡Óºá
			When you add an object to a HashMap / HashSet:
				Java calls hashCode()
				Hash code is converted into a bucket index
				Object is stored in that bucket
			
	=> ≡Óõ╣ How equals() works INTERNALLY ≡Óºá
			Used only when needed:
				Java first checks hashCode()
				If hashCodes are different ΓÁÈ objects are NOT equal
				If hashCodes are same ΓÁÈ Java calls equals()
			
		=> if (obj1.hashCode() == obj2.hashCode()) {
				obj1.equals(obj2);
			}
			
	≡Óõ╣ Contract Between equals() and hashCode() ≡Óô£
			Java enforces this contract:
				1∩╕Â. If a.equals(b) is true
				Γ₧í∩╕Â a.hashCode() == b.hashCode() must be true

				2∩╕Â. If hashCode() is same
				Γ₧í∩╕Â equals() may be true or false

				ΓÙÔ Breaking this contract causes bugs in HashMap / HashSet
				
	==> Γ£õ∩╕Â Statement 1 : If equals() returns true, then hashCode() must be the same
	==> Γ£õ∩╕Â Statement 1 : If two objects have the same hashCode, equals() may return true or false
	
	

127. ≡Óõ╣ Runnable vs Callable :

		| Feature      | Runnable | Callable |
		| ------------ | -------- | -------- |
		| Return value | ΓÙÔ No     | Γ£à Yes    |
		| Exception    | ΓÙÔ No     | Γ£à Yes    |
		| Method       | `run()`  | `call()` |


128. ≡Óõ╣ Core Components of ExecutorService ≡Óºá
		1∩╕Â. Task :
			Runnable ΓÁÈ no result
			Callable<V> ΓÁÈ returns result & can throw exception
			
		2. Thread Pool :
			A set of reusable worker threads
			Types:
				Fixed thread pool
				Cached thread pool
				Single thread executor
				Scheduled executor
				
		3. Task Queue :
				Holds tasks when all threads are busy
				FIFO by default
				
129. what is dead lock and How to AVOID Deadlock Γ£à (MOST IMPORTANT)

	=> A deadlock occurs when two or more threads are permanently blocked, each waiting for a resource held by the other
	
		≡ÓÀÊ None of the threads can proceed
		≡ÓÀÊ Application appears hung forever
		
	=> ≡Óõ╣ How to AVOID Deadlock Γ£à (MOST IMPORTANT)
	
	1. Use tryLock() (Timeout-based locking) :
	
		Lock lock1 = new ReentrantLock();
		Lock lock2 = new ReentrantLock();

		if (lock1.tryLock()) {
			try {
				if (lock2.tryLock()) {
					try {
						// work
					} finally {
						lock2.unlock();
					}
				}
			} finally {
				lock1.unlock();
			}
		}

	2. Minimize Lock Scope :
		synchronized(this) {
			// only critical code
		}
	
	3. Avoid Nested Locks (if possible) :
		synchronized(a) {
			synchronized(b) { }
		}

	4. Use Higher-Level Concurrency APIs :
		=> ExecutorService
		=> ConcurrentHashMap
		=> BlockingQueue
		
130. Can we synchronize constructor?
		=> A constructorΓÇÕs job is to create and initialize an object.
		=> During constructor execution:
				The object is not fully created
				The monitor lock (this) does not yet exist safely
				No other thread can access the object until construction finishes
				
			=> So synchronization makes no sense here
			
		=> but, use a synchronized block inside constructor
		
131. ≡ÓõÑ synchronized vs ReentrantLock :

	≡Óõ╣ What is synchronized :
		=> synchronized is a Java keyword that provides implicit locking using the JVMΓÇÕs intrinsic (monitor) lock.
		
		Eg:	synchronized void increment() {
				count++;
			}
			
		=> Lock is automatically acquired & released
		=> Simple and safe
		=> Managed entirely by JVM
		
	≡Óõ╣ What is ReentrantLock :
		=> ReentrantLock is a class from java.util.concurrent.locks package that provides explicit locking with advanced features.
		
		Eg : Lock lock = new ReentrantLock();

			lock.lock();
			try {
				count++;
			} finally {
				lock.unlock();
			}
			
			=> Locking is manual
			=> More control
			=> More powerful

132. CompletableFuture vs @Async ?

	≡Óõ╣ What is @Async?
			@Async is a Spring annotation used to run a method asynchronously in a separate thread.

		Eg :	@Async
				public void sendEmail() {
					// runs in background thread
				}

		≡Óõì How it works internally
			=> Spring creates a proxy
			=> Method is submitted to a TaskExecutor
			=> Caller thread does not wait
			
	≡Óõ╣ What is CompletableFuture?
			CompletableFuture is a Java 8 concurrency API for asynchronous programming with chaining.

			CompletableFuture
				.supplyAsync(() -> getUser())
				.thenApply(user -> user.getEmail())
				.thenAccept(System.out::println);

			≡Óõì How it works
				=> Executes tasks asynchronously
				=> Supports non-blocking pipelines
				=> Can combine multiple async tasks
				

133. PUT vs PATCH :

		| Aspect      | **PUT**                 | **PATCH**                 |  PUT : If a field is missing ΓÁÈ it may be deleted or reset
		| ----------- | ----------------------- | ------------------------- |  PATCH : updates only specified fields
		| Purpose     | Replace entire resource | Partially update resource |		   Unmentioned fields remain unchanged
		| Update type | Full update             | Partial update            |
		| Idempotent  | Γ£à Yes                   | Γ£à Yes (usually)           |
		| Payload     | Complete object         | Only changed fields       |
		| Risk        | Can overwrite fields    | Safer for partial changes |
						
		≡Óõ╣ REST Best Practices (Important)

		| Scenario             | Recommended |
		| -------------------- | ----------- |
		| Update full resource | PUT         |
		| Update single field  | PATCH       |
		| Toggle status        | PATCH       |
		| Reset entire object  | PUT         |
		

134. What is an Indexes ?

		≡Óõ╣ What is an Index?
		=> An index is a data structure used by a database to quickly locate rows without scanning the entire table.
		
		
		≡Óõ╣ Pros of Indexes Γ£à
			1. Faster SELECT Queries
			2. Faster Sorting & Filtering
			3. Faster JOIN Operations
			4. Enforces Uniqueness
			
		≡Óõ╣ Cons of Indexes ΓÙÔ
			1. Slower INSERT / UPDATE / DELETE : Every data change must update indexes.
			2. Extra Storage Space :
				=> Indexes consume disk & memory
				=> Large tables ΓÁÈ large indexes
			3. Over-Indexing Hurts Performance
			4. Maintenance Cost
	
135. Simplest & Correct Interview Version of Overiding equlas() and hashCode()

	public class Employee{
		private int id;
		private String name;
		
		@Override
		public boolean equals(Object obj){
			if(this == obj){
				return true;
			}
			
			if(!(Obj isntance Employee)){
				return false;
			}
			
			Employee e = (Employee) obj;
			return id == e.id && name.equals(e.name);
		}
		
		@Override
		public int hashCode(){
			return id + name.hashCode();
		}
		
	}
	
	
136. SOLID is a set of 5 object-oriented design principles that help you build clean, maintainable, and scalable software:

	1. SRP ΓÇô Single Responsibility Principle : SRP means one class should handle one responsibility
	2. OCP ΓÇô OpenΓÇôClosed Principle : Classes should be open for extension but closed for modification
	3. LSP ΓÇô Liskov Substitution Principle : Objects of a subclass should be replaceable with objects of the superclass.
	4. ISP ΓÇô Interface Segregation Principle : Clients should not be forced to implement methods they do not use.
	5. DIP ΓÇô Dependency Inversion Principle : High-level modules should not depend on low-level modules. Both should depend on abstractions.
		
	
137. LSP Violation Example (Bird)  :

	ΓÙÔ Wrong Design
		class Bird {
			void fly() { }
		}

		class Penguin extends Bird {
			@Override
			void fly() {
				throw new UnsupportedOperationException();
			}
		}

		Usage :
			Bird bird = new Penguin();
			bird.fly(); // ≡ÓÈÑ Runtime error

		≡ÓÜ¿ Penguin is not substitutable for Bird
		≡ÓÜ¿ Violates LSP


138. ≡Óº╡ Thread Life Cycle in Java :

	=> In Java, a thread goes through several well-defined states during its lifetime. 
		These states are defined in the Thread.State enum.
	
	1. NEW :
		=> A thread is in the NEW state after it is created but before it is started.
		=> It has not yet begun execution.

		=> Thread t = new Thread();
			Γ£õ Thread object created
			ΓÙÔ start() not called yet
		
	2. RUNNABLE :
		=> After calling start(), the thread moves to RUNNABLE.
		=> It is ready to run and waiting for CPU time.
		=> It may be running or ready to run (Java does not distinguish between ready and running).

		=> t.start();
			Γ£õ Eligible for execution
			Γ£õ Controlled by the thread scheduler
		
	3. BLOCKED :
		=> A thread enters BLOCKED state when it is waiting to acquire a monitor lock.
		=> Happens when trying to enter a synchronized block/method that is already locked by another thread.

			synchronized(obj) {
			   // critical section
			}

			Γ£õ Waiting for lock
			ΓÙÔ Cannot proceed until lock is available
			
			
	4. WAITING :
		=> The thread waits indefinitely until another thread performs a specific action.
		=> Triggered by methods like:

			wait()
			join()
			LockSupport.park()

			Γ£õ No CPU usage
			Γ£õ Waiting for notification
			
	5. TIMED_WAITING :
		=> The thread waits for a specified amount of time.

		=> Triggered by:
			sleep(1000)
			wait(1000)
			join(1000)

			Γ£õ Waiting with timeout
			Γ£õ Automatically returns after time expires
			
	6. TERMINATED
		=> The thread enters TERMINATED after execution completes.
		=> It cannot be restarted.

			=> // run() method finishes

			Γ£õ Thread is dead
			ΓÙÔ Cannot call start() again
			
	≡ÓÃ» Important Notes :
		=> A thread can move between RUNNABLE ΓÁõ BLOCKED/WAITING/TIMED_WAITING multiple times.
		=> Once in TERMINATED, it cannot restart.
		=> The thread scheduler controls execution order.
		
		
139. ≡ÓõÑ Difference Between volatile and synchronized in Java ?

	Γ£à  volatile :
			What it does:
				=> Ensures visibility of variable changes across threads.
				=> Prevents instruction reordering.
				=> Does NOT provide locking.
				=> Does NOT guarantee atomicity.

			Example:
				=> volatile boolean flag = false;

				If Thread A changes flag = true,
				Thread B immediately sees the updated value.

			ΓÙÔ But This Is Not Safe:
				volatile int count = 0;
				count++;   // Not atomic!
				=> Still causes race conditions.
				
	Γ£à synchronized :
			What it does:
			=> Provides mutual exclusion (locking).
			=> Ensures only one thread executes the block/method at a time.
			=> Guarantees visibility + atomicity.

			Example:
				synchronized void increment() {
					count++;
				}

				Γ£õ Thread-safe
				Γ£õ No race condition
				

140. If I want to write a thread safe code, what all things I should consider ?

	=> Writing thread-safe code is not just about adding synchronized. ItΓÇÕs about designing your code 
	   so that multiple threads can access shared data safely without causing race conditions, 
	   visibility issues, or deadlocks.
	   
	 Γ£à Identify Shared Mutable State (Most Important) :
			Ask yourself:
				=> Is this variable accessed by multiple threads?
				=> Can it change (mutable)?
				=> Is it read + written by different threads?
				
			≡ÓÀÊ If yes, it needs protection.
				eg: int count = 0;   // Shared + Mutable = Risky
				
				
	Γ£à Prefer Immutability (Best Strategy) :
			=> Immutable objects are automatically thread-safe.

			Eg:	final class User {
					private final String name;
					private final int age;

					public User(String name, int age) {
						this.name = name;
						this.age = age;
					}
				}
				
				Γ£õ No setters
				Γ£õ All fields final
				Γ£õ No synchronization needed

			≡ÓÈí Best practice: Make classes immutable whenever possible.
			
			
	Γ£à Use Proper Synchronization for Shared Data :
			=> If multiple operations must happen together, use:
			Γ£õ synchronized
					synchronized(this) {
						count++;
					}

			Γ£õ Locks
					Lock lock = new ReentrantLock();
					lock.lock();
					try {
						count++;
					} finally {
						lock.unlock();
					}

			Γ£õ Atomic Classes
					AtomicInteger count = new AtomicInteger(0);
					count.incrementAndGet();
				
	Γ£à Use volatile Only for Visibility (Not Locking) :
			=> Use volatile when:
				=> Only simple read/write
				=> No compound operations
			
			Good example:
			eg: volatile boolean shutdown;

			Bad example:
			eg:	volatile int count;
				count++;   // Still unsafe
				
	Γ£à Avoid Deadlocks :
			Deadlock happens when:
				=> Thread A ΓÁÈ holds Lock1 ΓÁÈ waiting for Lock2
				=> Thread B ΓÁÈ holds Lock2 ΓÁÈ waiting for Lock1

			Prevention:
				=> Always acquire locks in the same order
				=> Keep synchronized blocks small
				=> Avoid nested locks when possible
				
	Γ£à Minimize Scope of Synchronization :
			Bad:
			Eg:	public synchronized void method() {
					// 100 lines of code
				}

			Better:
			Eg:	public void method() {
					// non-critical code

					synchronized(this) {
						// only critical section
					}
				}

			Γ£õ Improves performance
			Γ£õ Reduces contention
			
	Γ£à Prefer High-Level Concurrency Utilities :
			Instead of manual threads, use:
				=> ExecutorService
				=> ConcurrentHashMap
				=> BlockingQueue
				=> AtomicInteger
				=> CompletableFuture
			These are safer and more scalable.
			
			
141. visibility vs atomcity vs ordering ?

	≡Óõì Visibility
		≡ÓÀÊ Meaning:
			When one thread updates a variable, will other threads see the updated value immediately?

		ΓÙÔ Without Visibility Guarantee :
				=> Each thread may cache variables locally.
					eg:	boolean flag = false;

						Thread A:
						flag = true;

						Thread B:
						while (!flag) { }   // may loop forever


				=> Thread B may never see the update.

		Γ£à How to Guarantee Visibility
				=> volatile
				=> synchronized
				=> Lock
				=> Atomic classes

					eg: volatile boolean flag;

				Now changes become visible to all threads immediately.
				
	ΓÜ¢ Atomicity
			≡ÓÀÊ Meaning:
					=> An operation happens completely or not at all (indivisible).
					=> No other thread can see it halfway.

			ΓÙÔ Not Atomic
				count++;   // NOT atomic

				This is actually:
					=> Read
					=> Increment
					=> Write

			Two threads can interfere ΓÁÈ wrong result.

			Γ£à Atomic Operations

				=> synchronized
				=> Lock
				=> AtomicInteger.incrementAndGet()

				Eg: AtomicInteger count = new AtomicInteger();
					count.incrementAndGet(); 
					
	≡Óõã Ordering
		≡ÓÀÊ Meaning:
				=> The execution order of instructions may be rearranged (reordered) by:
					1. Compiler
					2. JVM
					3. CPU

		For performance optimization:
		
				=> Example of Reordering
						int a = 1;
						int b = 2;

				=> The CPU might execute:
						b = 2;
						a = 1;

				=> Single thread ΓÁÈ no problem
				=> Multiple threads ΓÁÈ can cause serious bugs
				

	=> ≡Óºá Summary Table :
	
		| Concept    | What It Solves                  | Example Tool                    |
		| ---------- | ------------------------------- | ------------------------------- |
		| Visibility | Other threads see latest value  | `volatile`                      |
		| Atomicity  | Operation cannot be interrupted | `synchronized`, `AtomicInteger` |
		| Ordering   | Prevent instruction reordering  | `volatile`, `synchronized`      |
		

142. ≡Óº╡ What is a BlockingQueue in Java?

	=> A BlockingQueue is a thread-safe queue used in producerΓÇôconsumer scenarios where:
			≡Óõ╣ Producers add elements
			≡Óõ╣ Consumers remove elements
			≡Óõ╣ Threads automatically wait (block) when needed
		=> It is part of JavaΓÇÕs concurrency utilities (java.util.concurrent).

	Γ£à Why "Blocking"?
			Because operations block the thread when:
				1. Queue is empty ΓÁÈ take() waits
				2. Queue is full ΓÁÈ put() waits

			=> So you donΓÇÕt need to manually use:
				1. wait()
				2. notify()
				3. synchronized
				
		Eg: BlockingQueue<Integer> queue = new ArrayBlockingQueue<>(2);

				// Producer
				new Thread(() -> {
					try {
						queue.put(1);
						queue.put(2);
						queue.put(3); // waits (queue full) -> Queue size = 2
					} catch (InterruptedException e) {
						e.printStackTrace();
					}
				}).start();

				// Consumer
				new Thread(() -> {
					try {
						Thread.sleep(2000);
						System.out.println(queue.take()); // removes 1 
					} catch (Exception e) {}
				}).start();


143. ReentrantLock vs readWriteLock ?

	| Feature                  | ReentrantLock   | ReadWriteLock          |
	| ------------------------ | --------------- | ---------------------- |
	| Lock type                | Single lock     | Two locks (read/write) |
	| Multiple readers allowed | ΓÙÔ No            | Γ£à Yes                |
	| Multiple writers allowed | ΓÙÔ No            | ΓÙÔ No                 |
	| Best for                 | General locking | Read-heavy systems     |
	| Complexity               | Simple          | Slightly complex       |
	

144. If I want to call third party API, what things I should consider

		=>  When calling a third-party API, I consider timeout configuration, error handling, 
			retries with backoff, circuit breaker, rate limiting, security (authentication), 
			proper logging/monitoring, and validation of responses to prevent cascading failures.

147. Write a java program to find sum of even numbers and sum of odd numbers in a given list using java 8 streams.
		
		List<Integer> numbers = Arrays.asList(10, 123, 45, 1567, 89, 1002, 9);

        Map<Boolean, List<Integer>> collect = numbers.stream().collect(partitioningBy(n -> n % 2 == 0));
        System.out.println(collect.get(true));

        System.out.println(numbers.stream().filter(n-> n%2 == 0).reduce(0, (a,b) -> a+b));

        System.out.println(numbers.stream().filter(n-> n%2 == 0).mapToInt(Integer::intValue).sum());

152. Java Memory Model :
		Java Memory Model defines the rules by which threads interact through memory, 
		ensuring visibility, ordering, and atomicity in concurrent Java programs.

153. What is a Thread Dump? How Do You Analyze It? 
		=> A thread dump is a snapshot of all threads in a Java process at a specific moment.
		=> It shows what each thread is doing, its state, and locks it holds or is waiting for.

	=> Thread dumps are one of the most powerful production debugging tools for:
			1. Application hangs
			2. High CPU usage
			3. Deadlocks
			4. Performance bottlenecks

	=> How to Take a Thread Dump
			-> Using jstack
				 jstack <pid>
			-> Kill signal (Linux)
				 kill -3 <pid>


154. Diff between Docker and Docker compose 
	=> Docker runs containers, while Docker Compose defines and runs multi-container applications using a single configuration file.
	
	| Feature           | Docker          | Docker Compose              |
	| ----------------- | --------------- | --------------------------- |
	| Purpose           | Run containers  | Manage multi-container apps |
	| Configuration     | CLI commands    | YAML file                   |
	| Containers        | Single / manual | Multiple / automated        |
	| Networking        | Manual          | Auto-created                |
	| Best for          | Simple apps     | Microservices               |
	| Environment setup | Repetitive      | One-time config             |
	
	≡Óºá Real-World Example (Very Important)
		ΓÙÔ Without Docker Compose
			docker run mysql
			docker run redis
			docker run backend
			docker run frontend

		ΓÙÔ Hard to manage
		ΓÙÔ Order matters
		ΓÙÔ Error-prone

	Γ£à With Docker Compose
		docker-compose up

	Γ£õ Everything starts together
	Γ£õ Correct order
	Γ£õ Easy to stop: docker-compose down.

	≡ÓÉ│ Docker
	Docker is the engine that:
		Builds images
		Runs containers
		Pushes / pulls images
	=> But Docker does NOT decide where images are stored.
	
	≡Óôª What is a Docker Registry?
			A registry is a place where Docker images live.
		Examples:
			Docker Hub (default public registry)
			GitLab Container Registry
			Amazon ECR
			Azure Container Registry

						.uri(URI.create("https://example.com"))
						.build();

				HttpResponse<String> response =

			Γ£õ Lower latency

	≡ÓõÑ 7. Security & Performance Enhancements

			=> Reduced memory footprint.
			
			
157. What is SSO?

	Single Sign-On (SSO) lets a user log in once and access multiple applications without logging in again.
	
	Eg : Login to Company Portal ΓÁÈ Access Email, HR, Jira, GitLab automatically.
	
	≡Óõã SSO Login Flow (OAuth 2.0 / OpenID Connect) :
	Γ£à Step-by-Step Flow
	
	1. User accesses Application
		User ΓÁÈ App
			User opens the app (e.g., HR portal).
		
	2. App redirects to Identity Provider
		App ΓÁÈ IdP (Login Page)
			Because the user is not authenticated, the app redirects to the IdP.
		
	3. User authenticates at IdP
		User ΓÁÈ IdP
			User enters credentials (password, OTP, biometrics).
			Γ£õ Authentication happens only at IdP
			
	4. IdP issues tokens
		IdP generates:
			ID Token (who the user is)
			Access Token (permissions)
			
	5. Tokens sent back to Application
		IdP ΓÁÈ App (via redirect)
		
	6. App validates token
			Signature
			Expiry
			Issuer
		Γ£õ If valid ΓÁÈ user is logged in
		
	7. User accesses other apps (SSO magic Γ£¿)
		User ΓÁÈ Another App ΓÁÈ IdP ΓÁÈ Already logged in Γ£õ
			No login screen again
			
	≡ÓÃ» Interview One-Liner (Must Remember):
	
			=> In SSO, authentication is handled by a central Identity Provider. Once authenticated, 
			   the IdP issues a token that allows the user to access multiple applications without logging in again.
			   
			   
158. ≡Óºá Where credentials are stored in SSO

	≡Óõ╣ Identity Provider (IdP) ΓÇô Γ£à YES
			User credentials are stored centrally in the IdPΓÇÕs user store.
		Examples of IdPs:
			Keycloak
			Okta
			Azure Active Directory
			Active Directory
			
			
159. @primary and @qualifier how and when to use ?

	≡Óºá The Problem They Solve
		=> When multiple beans of the same type exist in Spring, Spring doesnΓÇÕt know which one to inject.

	eg:	interface PaymentService {
			void pay();
		}

	1.	@Service
		class CardPaymentService implements PaymentService { }

	2.	@Service
		class UpiPaymentService implements PaymentService { }

	3.	@Autowired
		PaymentService paymentService; // ΓÙÔ Ambiguous

	≡Óõ╣ @Primary ΓÇõ Default Bean
		≡ÓôÔ What it does
			=> Marks one bean as the default choice when multiple candidates exist.

		Γ£à Example :
		
			@Service
			@Primary
			class CardPaymentService implements PaymentService { }

			@Autowired
			PaymentService paymentService; // Injects CardPaymentService

			Γ£õ Use @Primary when
				=> One implementation is used most of the time
				=> You want a default behavior
				=> You want less configuration
				
	≡Óõ╣ @Qualifier ΓÇõ Explicit Selection 
	
			≡ÓôÔ What it does
				=> Tells Spring exactly which bean to inject.

			Γ£à Example
				@Service("cardPayment")
				class CardPaymentService implements PaymentService { }

				@Service("upiPayment")
				class UpiPaymentService implements PaymentService { }

				@Autowired
				@Qualifier("upiPayment")
				PaymentService paymentService;

			Γ£õ Use @Qualifier when
					=> You need different implementations in different places
					=> Behavior changes based on context
					=> You want clear & explicit wiring
					
	Note : if we use both : Γ£õ @Qualifier overrides @Primary
	
	
160. How HashSet works internally (MOST IMPORTANT) 

	=> Γ£à Key fact (Interview must-know)
			HashSet is internally backed by a HashMap
			
	eg: ≡ÓºÒ What happens when you add an element?
	
				Set<String> set = new HashSet<>();
				set.add("Java");

			=> Internally:
					map.put("Java", PRESENT);
					
					Element ΓÁÈ key
					Dummy object ΓÁÈ value
					
		≡Óõã Step-by-step flow :
			1∩╕Â. hashCode() of element is called
			2∩╕Â. Bucket index is calculated
			3∩╕Â. If bucket empty ΓÁÈ element stored
			4∩╕Â. If collision ΓÁÈ equals() is used
			5∩╕Â. If equal ΓÁÈ ΓÙÔ duplicate (ignored)
			6∩╕Â. If not equal ΓÁÈ stored in same bucket
			
	Note : ΓÙùWhy duplicates are not allowed ?
			Because:
				hashCode() + equals()
				determine uniqueness
				
161. If two Employee objects have the same ID & name, will a Set allow duplicates?
	
	≡ÓÀÊ It depends on whether equals() and hashCode() are overridden correctly.
	
	≡Óõ┤ Case 1: equals() & hashCode() NOT overridden ΓÙÔ
			class Employee {
				int id;
				String name;
			}

			Set<Employee> set = new HashSet<>();
			set.add(new Employee(1, "John"));
			set.add(new Employee(1, "John"));
			
	ΓÙÔ Result:
			Duplicates ARE allowed
			Because:
				Default equals() ΓÁÈ compares memory references
				Different objects ΓÁÈ different references
				
				
	≡ÓÓó Case 2: equals() & hashCode() overridden correctly Γ£à
			class Employee {
				int id;
				String name;

				@Override
				public boolean equals(Object o) {
					if (this == o) return true;
					if (!(o instanceof Employee)) return false;
					Employee e = (Employee) o;
					return id == e.id && Objects.equals(name, e.name);
				}

				@Override
				public int hashCode() {
					return Objects.hash(id, name);
				}
			}

			Set<Employee> set = new HashSet<>();
			set.add(new Employee(1, "John"));
			set.add(new Employee(1, "John"));

			Γ£à Result:
				Duplicate is NOT allowed
				Second add() is ignored
				

162. Difference between @ExceptionHandler and @ControllerAdvice :

	1. @ExceptionHandler handles exceptions locally (inside a controller).
	2. @ControllerAdvice makes exception handling global (across all controllers).
	
	Eg1 ≡Óõ╣ @ExceptionHandler
			≡ÓôÔ What it is

			A method-level annotation used inside a controller to handle exceptions thrown by that controller only.

			Γ£à Example
			@RestController
			@RequestMapping("/users")
			public class UserController {

				@GetMapping("/{id}")
				public String getUser(@PathVariable int id) {
					if (id == 0) {
						throw new IllegalArgumentException("Invalid ID");
					}
					return "User";
				}

	Imp :		@ExceptionHandler(IllegalArgumentException.class)
				public ResponseEntity<String> handleIllegalArg(IllegalArgumentException ex) {
					return ResponseEntity
							.badRequest()
							.body(ex.getMessage());
				}
			}

			≡Óõì Scope
				ΓÙÔ Only this controller
				ΓÙÔ Not reusable
				Γ£à Simple for small controllers
				
	
	Eg2 :≡Óõ╣ @ControllerAdvice
			≡ÓôÔ What it is

			A cross-cutting component that applies to all controllers (global exception handling).

			Γ£à Example
			@ControllerAdvice
			public class GlobalExceptionHandler {

				@ExceptionHandler(IllegalArgumentException.class)
				public ResponseEntity<String> handleIllegalArg(IllegalArgumentException ex) {
					return ResponseEntity
							.badRequest()
							.body(ex.getMessage());
				}

				@ExceptionHandler(Exception.class)
				public ResponseEntity<String> handleAll(Exception ex) {
					return ResponseEntity
							.status(HttpStatus.INTERNAL_SERVER_ERROR)
							.body("Something went wrong");
				}
			}

			≡Óõì Scope
				Γ£à All controllers
				Γ£à Reusable
				Γ£à Clean & centralized
				
				
163. Input: primitive 
		int If 1 ΓÁÈ return 0 
		    If 0 ΓÁÈ return 1 
		ΓÙÔ Without using if or switch
		
		Eg 1:  int input = 1  ;
			   int output = input^1;
			   System.out.println(output); 
			   
		Eg2 : int output = 1 - input;
		
		
164. If Interface A and B both have default void multiply(), which one executes in MyClass?
	
	Eg: interface A {
    default void multiply() {
        System.out.println("A multiply");
    }
	}

	interface B {
		default void multiply() {
			System.out.println("B multiply");
		}
	}

	class MyClass implements A, B {
	}
	ΓÙÔ Compile-time Error
	
	Solutions :
	
	1. Override in MyClass :
		@Override
		public void multiply() {
			System.out.println("MyClass multiply");
		}
		
	2. ≡ÓõÑ Calling a Specific Interface Method (IMPORTANT)
		@Override
		public void multiply() {
			A.super.multiply(); // calls A's method
			// B.super.multiply(); // OR call B's
		}
		
	3. Γ£à Rule 1: Class > Interface :
		If a class already has multiply():
			class Parent {
				public void multiply() {}
			}

		Γ₧í Interface default methods are ignored
		
	4. Rule 2: More Specific Interface Wins
			interface A {
				default void multiply() {}
			}

			interface B extends A {
			}

			Γ₧í BΓÇÕs default method is chosen
			
			
165. How to trace issues across microservices logs? 

	=> Use a Correlation ID / Trace ID (FOUNDATION)
			What it is
				=> A unique ID per request that every service logs.

				How it flows
				Client ΓÁÈ API Gateway ΓÁÈ Service A ΓÁÈ Service B ΓÁÈ DB
						  traceId=abc123 everywhere

				Example (log line)
				2026-02-18 10:12:45 INFO traceId=abc123 service=order msg="Order created"


		Flow:	Client
				  ΓÁô
				API Gateway (creates traceId=abc123)
				  ΓÁô (traceparent header)
				Order Service (spanId=1)
				  ΓÁô (same traceId, new spanId)
				Payment Service (spanId=2)
				  ΓÁô
				Database
				
				
166. Count uppercase & lowercase characters
	
	int upper = 0, lower = 0;

	for (char c : input.toCharArray()) {
		if (Character.isUpperCase(c)) upper++;
		else if (Character.isLowerCase(c)) lower++;
	}

	System.out.println("Uppercase: " + upper);
	System.out.println("Lowercase: " + lower);
	
	
167. What is try-with-resources and how AutoCloseable works internally?

	ΓÙÔ Old way (before Java 7)
	
			BufferedReader br = null;
			try {
				br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("file.txt"));
				System.out.println(br.readLine());
			} finally {
				if (br != null) {
					br.close(); // easy to forget / error-prone
				}
			}


			Problems:
				Boilerplate code
				close() may be forgotten
				Exception handling is messy
				
	Γ£à try-with-resources (Java 7+) :
	
			try (BufferedReader br =
					 new BufferedReader(new FileReader("file.txt"))) {

				System.out.println(br.readLine());

			} // br.close() is called automatically


			Γ£õ Cleaner
			Γ£õ Safer
			Γ£õ No memory / resource leaks
			
		
	Γ£à Final Takeaway
			=> try-with-resources = safe resource management
			=> Powered by AutoCloseable
			=> Compiler generates cleanup code
			=> Prevents leaks & lost exceptions
			=> Essential for DB, files, streams
			
			
168. How to find the number of tables and their columns in the SQL DB?

	=> just want to get the tables
			SELECT table_name
			FROM information_schema.tables
			WHERE table_schema = 'your_database_name';

	=>  Find tables with their column count
			SELECT table_name, COUNT(column_name) AS column_count
			FROM information_schema.columns
			WHERE table_schema = 'your_database_name'
			GROUP BY table_name;

169. Difference between REST and SOAP.

	=> REST is an architectural style that uses HTTP methods and mostly JSON for lightweight communication.
	=> SOAP is a protocol that uses XML-based messaging with strict standards like WS-Security.
	=> REST is faster and widely used in microservices, while SOAP is more secure and used in enterprise systems like banking.
	
	1. Security :
		REST ΓÁÈ Uses HTTPS, JWT, OAuth (like in Spring Security)
			SOAP ΓÁÈ Has built-in security standard called WS-Security
		SOAP is stronger in:
			Banking
			Payment gateways
			Enterprise integrations
			
	2. Stateless :
		REST ΓÁÈ Always stateless
		SOAP ΓÁÈ Can be stateful or stateless
		
	3. Error Handling :
		REST:
			Uses HTTP status codes (200, 404, 500)
		SOAP:
			Uses SOAP Fault (inside XML)
			
	4. Real World Usage :
		REST Used In:
			Twitter API
			Facebook Graph API
			Amazon web services

		SOAP Used In:
			Banking systems
			Payment gateways
			Legacy enterprise systems
			
			
170.  Γ¡É Why Spring 
		=> Spring is a lightweight Java framework that provides dependency injection and inversion of control 
		   to build loosely coupled, scalable, and maintainable enterprise applications.
		   
		=> We use Spring to reduce boilerplate code, manage object lifecycle, handle transactions, 
		   security, and build RESTful web services efficiently.

	  Γ¡É Why Spring Boot?
		=> Spring Boot is built on top of Spring and removes XML configuration by providing auto-configuration 
		   and embedded servers, making application development faster.
		   
171. How does Autowiring work in Spring?

		=> Autowiring is the process by which Spring automatically injects dependent objects (beans) into 
		   another bean at runtime.
		   
	
172. what is spring security ? 

		=> Spring Security is a framework that provides authentication, authorization, and protection 
		   against common security attacks for Spring applications.
		   
	=> What Problems Spring Security Solves :
		
		| Problem              | Solution                   |
		| -------------------- | -------------------------- |
		| Who are you?         | Authentication             |
		| What can you access? | Authorization              |
		| Password protection  | Password encoders          |
		| API security         | JWT / OAuth2               |
		| Attacks              | CSRF, CORS, XSS protection |

173. Difference between HTTP and HTTPS :

		=> HTTP is an unsecured protocol where data is transferred in plain text.
		=> HTTPS is the secure version of HTTP that uses SSL/TLS encryption to protect data, 
		   ensure server authenticity, and prevent tampering.
		   
174. Model class validations: 

	1. String Validations :
		@NotEmpty          // Not null AND length > 0
		@Size(min = 3, max = 20)
		@Pattern(regexp = "^[A-Za-z]+$")
		@Email
		
	2. Number Validations :
		@Min(18)
		@Max(60)
		@Positive -> eg: private int age;
		@PositiveOrZero
		@Negative
		@NegativeOrZero
		@Digits(integer = 5, fraction = 2)
		
	3. Date & Time Validations :
		@Past -> Date must be in past
		@PastOrPresent
		@Future -> Date must be in future
		@FutureOrPresent
		
	4. Boolean Validation :
		@AssertTrue
		@AssertFalse
		
175. Why does Object class contain wait(), notify(), and notifyAll()?

		=> wait(), notify(), and notifyAll() are in the Object class because they work at the object (monitor) 
		   level, not at the thread level, and every object in Java can act as a lock (monitor).
		   
		 => Core Concept: Monitor / Intrinsic Lock
			Every Java object has:
				=> Monitor (intrinsic lock)
				=> Used for thread synchronization
				=> synchronized keyword locks an object, not a thread
				
				≡ÓÀÊ Since locks belong to objects, these methods are placed in Object.
				
		=> What These Methods Do :
			| Method        | Purpose                       |
			| ------------- | ----------------------------- |
			| `wait()`      | Releases lock and waits       |
			| `notify()`    | Wakes **one** waiting thread  |
			| `notifyAll()` | Wakes **all** waiting threads |


176. Older and New way of read the files in java ?
	
	≡Óõ╣ Old Way (java.io): Java 1.1
	
		import java.io.BufferedReader;
		import java.io.FileReader;
		import java.io.IOException;

		BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("employees.csv"));
		String line;

		while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) {
			System.out.println(line);
		}
		br.close();
		
	Problems:
		More boilerplate
		Manual closing
		Not stream-friendly
		
		
	≡Óõ╣ Modern Way (java.nio): Java 7+
	
		import java.nio.file.Files;
		import java.nio.file.Paths;

		Files.lines(Paths.get("employees.csv"))
			 .forEach(System.out::println);

		Advantages:
			Cleaner code
			Returns Stream<String>
			Easily used with Stream API
			Can use try-with-resources

177. Read employee data from a CSV file and perform operations using Parallel Streams
	
	1. Read CSV & Convert to Employee List :
	
		import java.io.IOException;
		import java.nio.file.Files;
		import java.nio.file.Paths;
		import java.util.List;
		import java.util.stream.Collectors;

		public class EmployeeCSVReader {

			public static List<Employee> readEmployees(String filePath) throws IOException {

				return Files.lines(Paths.get(filePath))
						.skip(1) // skip header
						.map(line -> line.split(","))
						.map(data -> new Employee(
								Integer.parseInt(data[0]),
								data[1],
								data[2],
								Double.parseDouble(data[3])
						))
						.collect(Collectors.toList());
			}
		}
		
178. ≡ÓÜÇ Real-Time Scenarios Using Executors :

	1. Microservice Handling Multiple Requests :
			Scenario:
				You are building a Spring Boot microservice.
			Each request needs:
				DB call
				External API call
				Logging
				Instead of blocking main thread:

			ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(10);

			executor.submit(() -> callExternalService());

			Γ£à Improves throughput
			Γ£à Better CPU utilization

			≡ÓÀÊ Used in: Payment systems, Order processing systems

	2. Sending Bulk Emails / Notifications :
			Scenario:
				User uploads 10,000 email IDs.

			Without Executor:
				Sequential processing ΓÁÈ Slow
			
			With Executor:
				ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(5);

				for (String email : emailList) {
					executor.submit(() -> sendEmail(email));
				}


				Γ£à Parallel execution
				Γ£à Controlled threads

			Used in:
				E-commerce promotions
				OTP systems
				Marketing platforms

	3. File Processing / CSV Processing
			Scenario:
				Large CSV file with 1 million records.
				Split into chunks and process in parallel.

			ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(4);

			executor.submit(() -> processChunk(chunk1));
			executor.submit(() -> processChunk(chunk2));

			Γ£à Faster processing
			Γ£à CPU-core utilization
		
	==> What does newFixedThreadPool(4) mean ?
			=> 4 means ΓÁÈ maximum 4 threads will run tasks concurrently.
			
			=> At any point in time:
					Max 4 tasks can execute in parallel
					Remaining tasks wait in a queue
					
					
179. What is a Record class in Java?

	=> Records were introduced in Java 14 (preview) and became stable in Java 16.
	   They are a special kind of class designed to model immutable data carriers with very little 
	   boilerplate code.
	
	≡ÓÀÊ Think of records as transparent data holders.
	
	=> Why were Records introduced?
		  Before records, even simple data classes required a lot of code:
			fields
			constructor
			getters
			equals()
			hashCode()
			toString()
				=> Records generate all of this automatically.
				
	ΓÙÔ Normal class
			public class Employee {
				private final int id;
				private final String name;

				public Employee(int id, String name) {
					this.id = id;
					this.name = name;
				}

				public int getId() { return id; }
				public String getName() { return name; }

				// equals, hashCode, toString...
			}
	Γ£à Record
			public record Employee(int id, String name) {}

			ThatΓÇÕs it ≡ÓÀÁ
			Java automatically provides:
				private final fields
				canonical constructor
				getters (id(), name())
				equals(), hashCode(), toString()
				
	=> Can we inherit (extend) a Record class ?

		ΓÙÔ NO ΓÇõ Records cannot be extended

			public record Employee(int id, String name) {}

			class Manager extends Employee {   // ΓÙÔ Compilation error
			}

		≡ÓôÔ Reason:
			Records are implicitly final
			Java enforces immutability and structural equality
			Allowing inheritance would break these guarantees
			
	
180. What are Sealed Classes in Java?
		=> Why do we need Sealed Classes?
			Before sealed classes:
				Any class could extend your base class (unless it was final)
				This could break business rules or design constraints

			With sealed classes:
				You get controlled inheritance
				Better domain modeling
				Strong compile-time safety
				
	Eg:	Basic Syntax : 
	
			public sealed class Payment
					permits CreditCardPayment, UpiPayment, CashPayment {
			}

			Only the listed classes can extend Payment.
			

	Note : Sealed Interface ΓÇô Basic Example :
	
			public sealed interface Payment
					permits CardPayment, UpiPayment, CashPayment {
			}


			Only these classes can implement Payment.
