	List<String> sentaence = List.of("hello how are you", "I'm Good!");
        String collect = sentaence.stream().flatMap(n -> Arrays.stream(n.split(" "))).collect(joining(" "));
    System.out.println(collect);

182. What is CQRS (Command Query Responsibility Segregation) pattern ?

	=> CQRS is a pattern that separates read and write operations into different models to improve scalability, performance, and maintainability 
	
	≡Óõ╣ Key Characteristics
		1. Separate models :
				=> Write model ΓÁÈ Business logic
				=> Read model ΓÁÈ Optimized for UI
		2. Scalable :
				=> Scale read and write independently
		3. Performance :
				=> Reads use denormalized tables or views
		4. Clear responsibility :
				=> Commands = intent to change
				=> Queries = data retrieval only
				
	≡Óõ╣ CQRS vs CRUD
		| CRUD                     | CQRS                         |
		| ------------------------ | ---------------------------- |
		| One model for everything | Separate read & write models |
		| Simpler                  | More complex                 |
		| Hard to scale reads      | Easy to scale reads          |
		| Best for small apps      | Best for complex domains     |
		
		
183. If two threads are using shared resources, how do wait() and run() differ?

		=> wait() releases the lock and pauses the thread until notified, 
		   whereas run() contains the code that a thread executes and does not affect locking or thread state.
		   
	≡Óõ╣ Why wait() Exists (Shared Resource Scenario)  => RUNNABLE --wait()--> WAITING --notify()--> RUNNABLE
			When two threads share a resource, one thread may need to:
				Pause until a condition becomes true
				Allow other thread to modify shared data
				Avoid busy-waiting
		=> ThatΓÇÕs exactly what wait() does.
		
	≡Óõ╣ What run() Actually Does
		class MyTask implements Runnable {
			public void run() {
				System.out.println("Thread logic here");
			}
		}

		=> run() is just a method
		=> JVM executes run() when you call start()
		=> Calling run() directly ΓÙÔ does NOT create a new thread

184. how would you design a spring boot API to handle large file uploads while keeping the system responsive ?
	1. Enable Streaming (Avoid Loading Entire File in Memory)

	=> Better approach ΓÁÈ use InputStream streaming

		@PostMapping("/upload")
		public ResponseEntity<String> upload(HttpServletRequest request) throws IOException {
		    InputStream inputStream = request.getInputStream();
		    Files.copy(inputStream, Paths.get("temp-file.dat"), StandardCopyOption.REPLACE_EXISTING);
		    return ResponseEntity.accepted().body("File received");
		}
		
		Γ£õ Streams directly to disk
		Γ£õ No full memory loading

	2. Make Upload Processing Asynchronous
	=> DonΓÇÕt process file in the request thread.

			Use:
			@Async
				ExecutorService
				Message Queue (Kafka / RabbitMQ)
				Background job
			
			Example
			@Async
			public void processFile(Path filePath) {
			    // heavy processing
			}

	3.Use Chunked Upload (Very Important for Large Files) :
			Instead of uploading 2GB at once:
			Client uploads file in chunks:
			
			POST /upload?chunk=1
			POST /upload?chunk=2
			POST /upload?chunk=3
			
			Server:
			Stores chunks temporarily
			Reconstructs final file
			Process after all chunks received
				Γ£õ Prevents timeout
				Γ£õ Better fault tolerance

	4. Store File in Cloud (Best Practice)
			DonΓÇÕt store large files in:
				ΓÙÔ Database (bad idea)
				ΓÙÔ Local disk in production
			
			Use:
			AWS S3
			Azure Blob Storage
			GCP Storage
			
			Even better ≡ÓÀç
			≡ÓõÑ Direct Upload Strategy (Best Design)
			Instead of uploading through your backend:
			Backend generates pre-signed URL
			Client uploads directly to S3

185. working example of how caching is implemented in Spring Boot, how Spring knows what to cache, and where the cache is store :

	≡Óõ╣ What is Caching in Spring Boot?
			Caching means storing API results temporarily so that:
			Repeated requests return data faster
			Database calls are reduced
			System remains responsive under load
			Spring Boot provides caching via Spring Cache Abstraction.

	1. Enable Caching in Spring Boot :
		@EnableCaching
		@SpringBootApplication
		public class Application {
		    public static void main(String[] args) {
		        SpringApplication.run(Application.class, args);
		    }
		}
		
		≡ÓÀÊ This enables SpringΓÇÕs caching mechanism.

	2. Service Layer (IMPORTANT) :
			@Service
			public class EmployeeService {
			
			    @Cacheable(value = "employees", key = "#id")
			    public Employee getEmployeeById(int id) {
			
			        System.out.println("Fetching from DB...");
			        // Simulate DB call
			        return new Employee(id, "Raj", 50000);
			    }
			}

	≡ÓôÔ Flow:
			1. API called with id=1
			2. Spring checks cache:
				ΓÙÔ Not found ΓÁÈ call method ΓÁÈ store result
			3. Next call with id=1
				Γ£à Found in cache ΓÁÈ return cached value
				ΓÙÔ Method NOT executed

	=> Where Is Cache Data Stored?
		≡Óõ╣ Default (If You Do Nothing)
		
		≡ÓÀÊ In-memory cache (ConcurrentHashMap)
		Γ£õ Stored inside application memory
		ΓÙÔ Lost on restart
		ΓÙÔ Not suitable for distributed systems

186. What is a Composite Index?
	=> A composite index (also called a multi-column index) is an index created on two or more columns of a table.
	=> Instead of indexing a single column, it indexes the combination of columns.

187. What Are Isolation Levels?
	=> Isolation levels define how and when the changes made by one transaction become visible to other concurrent transactions.

	The 4 SQL Isolation Levels (Low ΓÁÈ High) :
	1.READ UNCOMMITTED (Lowest)
		≡ÓÀÊ A transaction can read uncommitted data from another transaction.
			ΓÙÔ Dirty Read
			ΓÙÔ Non-repeatable Read
			ΓÙÔ Phantom Read
		≡ÓôÔ Rarely used (unsafe)

	2. READ COMMITTED (Most Common ΓÇô Default in many DBs)
		≡ÓÀÊ Can read only committed data.
			Γ£à Dirty Read prevented
			ΓÙÔ Non-repeatable Read
			ΓÙÔ Phantom Read

	3.REPEATABLE READ
		≡ÓÀÊ Same row read multiple times gives same value.
			Γ£à Dirty Read prevented
			Γ£à Non-repeatable Read prevented
			ΓÙÔ Phantom Read (mostly*)

	4. SERIALIZABLE (Highest)
		≡ÓÀÊ Transactions behave as if executed one after another.
			Γ£à Dirty Read
			Γ£à Non-repeatable Read
			Γ£à Phantom Read

	≡ÓõÑ Real-World Example (Bank Account)
			Scenario:
			Two users reading & updating same account balance.
				READ COMMITTED ΓÁÈ balance may change between reads
				REPEATABLE READ ΓÁÈ balance remains same
				SERIALIZABLE ΓÁÈ strict locking, safest

188. What is Stateless and Statefull
	| Feature             | Stateless | Stateful         |
	| ------------------- | --------- | ---------------- |
	| Stores client state | ΓÙÔ No      | Γ£à Yes         |
	| Server memory usage | Low       | High             |
	| Scalability         | Easy      | Difficult        |
	| Failure recovery    | Easy      | Hard             |
	| Common use          | REST APIs | Traditional apps |


189. diff between offset and cursor based pagination

	| Feature         | Offset-Based          | Cursor-Based    |
	| --------------- | --------------------- | --------------- |
	| Pagination type | Page number           | Last-seen value |
	| Performance     | ΓÙÔ Slow for large data | Γ£à Fast          |
	| Consistency     | ΓÙÔ Can break           | Γ£à Stable        |
	| Jump to page    | Γ£à Yes                 | ΓÙÔ No            |
	| Best for        | Small datasets        | Large datasets  |
	| DB load         | High                  | Low             |


190. what is multithreading ? and how and where did you used in project ?
		=> Multithreading is a programming concept where multiple threads execute concurrently within a single process, sharing the same memory space.
				Each thread performs a part of the task, which helps in:
				Better CPU utilization
				Improved performance
				Faster response time
				Efficient handling of parallel tasks

	Eg : Multithreaded Bulk Email (Recommended)
			≡Óõ╣ Using ExecutorService
					ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(10);
					
					for (User user : users) {
					  Future<?> future =  executor.submit(() -> emailService.sendEmail(user));
					}
					
					executor.shutdown();

				=> Now you can check task status.
						future.isDone();     // check if completed
						future.get();        // wait until task completes

					Γ£õ Sends multiple emails in parallel
					Γ£õ Controlled concurrency (10 threads)
					Γ£õ Faster processing

191. Diff @Async and Executor Service :

		| Feature                 | ExecutorService     | @Async                    |
		| ----------------------- | ------------------- | ------------------------- |
		| Belongs to              | Java                | Spring                    |
		| Thread pool management  | Manual              | Auto (Spring-managed)     |
		| Boilerplate             | More                | Less                      |
		| Best for                | Complex concurrency | Service-layer async tasks |
		| Integration with Spring | No                  | Yes                       |


192. Diff between @Async and CompletableFuture
	| Aspect                 | `@Async`                              | `CompletableFuture` |
	| ---------------------- | ------------------------------------- | ------------------- |
	| Level                  | Spring abstraction                    | Core Java           |
	| Return value           | `void`, `Future`, `CompletableFuture` | `CompletableFuture` |
	| Chaining               | ΓÙÔ Limited                             | Γ£à Very powerful     |
	| Combine multiple tasks | ΓÙÔ No                                  | Γ£à Yes               |
	| Error handling         | Basic                                 | Advanced            |
	| Best for               | Background tasks                      | Parallel workflows  |

193. Advanctage and disadvabtages of Record class ?
	Advantages:
		=> Compact syntax.
		=> Automatic generation of methods like toString(), equals(), and hashCode().
		=> Immutability by default.
		=> Ideal for data transfer and simple data-holding objects.

	Drawbacks:
		=> No inheritance (cannot extend other classes).
		=> No setters for mutability.
		=> Limited constructor flexibility.
		=> Less suitable for complex behaviors or inheritance-based designs.

194. What volatile Means in Java (Spring Boot) ?
		eg:  private volatile boolean running = true;

		=> It guarantees:
			Γ£à Changes made by one thread are visible to other threads immediately
			Γ£à The value is always read from main memory (not thread cache)
			ΓÙÔ It does NOT make operations atomic

	=> what happens when non valatile varible is accessed by the mutliple thread ? 
		When a non-volatile variable is accessed by multiple threads, you can get:
			ΓÙÔ Visibility problems
			ΓÙÔ Race conditions
			ΓÙÔ Out-of-order execution issues
			ΓÙÔ Infinite loops


195. Spring Bean lifecycle ?
	=> In a Spring Boot application, the Spring Bean lifecycle describes how a bean is:
		Created ΓÁÈ Initialized ΓÁÈ Used ΓÁÈ Destroyed
	1, Bean Creation Phase
	2. Dependency Injection Phase => Injects dependencies (@Autowired, constructor injection, etc.)
	3. Initialization
		Spring calls initialization methods:
			@PostConstruct
			afterPropertiesSet() (optional)
			Custom init method (optional)
	4. Bean Ready to Use
			Bean is available for application logic.
	5. Bean Destruction
		When application stops:
			@PreDestroy
			destroy() method (optional)

	=> Spring Bean life cycle starts when the container creates the bean, injects dependencies, and calls initialization methods like @PostConstruct. 
	   The bean is then ready for use. When the application shuts down, Spring calls destroy methods such as @PreDestroy.

196. Diff between the @PostConstruct and init method
		1. @PostConstruct
				@PostConstruct
				public void init() {
				    System.out.println("Initialized using PostConstruct");
				}
	
				Γ£õ Automatically called after dependency injection
				Γ£õ Clean and simple
				Γ£õ Mostly used in Spring Boot projects

			2. init-method
					=> Configured externally.
	
					@Bean(initMethod = "customInit")
					public MyService myService() {
					    return new MyService();
					}
					
					public void customInit() {
					    System.out.println("Initialized using init-method");
					}
	
					Γ£õ No need to use annotation inside class
					Γ£õ Useful if class is from third-party library

		=> @PostConstruct is an annotation used inside the bean class to define an initialization method.
			init-method is defined in Spring configuration and does not require modifying the class.
			In modern Spring Boot projects, @PostConstruct is commonly used.

197. SOLID Principles (Practical + Interview Ready)
		SOLID =
		Single Responsibility
		Open/Closed
		Liskov Substitution
		Interface Segregation
		Dependency Inversion

	1 Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)
			Definition:
				=> A class should have only one reason to change.
					Γ£õ Where I used it in project
					In my Spring Boot project, I separated business logic, persistence, and validation into different classes.	

		ΓÙÔ Bad Example
				class UserService {
				    void saveUser() {}
				    void sendEmail() {}
				    void generateReport() {}
				}
		Γ£à Good Example
				class UserService {
				    void saveUser() {}
				}
				
				class EmailService {
				    void sendEmail() {}
				}
				
				class ReportService {
				    void generateReport() {}
				}
				
				≡ÓôÔ Interview line:
					I followed SRP by separating services so that each class handled only one responsibility.

	2. Open/Closed Principle (OCP) 
			Definition:
				=> Classes should be open for extension but closed for modification.
				=> We used interfaces and strategy pattern so new behavior can be added without changing existing code.

		Eg : public interface NotificationService {
		    void send(String message);
		}
		@Service
		public class EmailNotificationService implements NotificationService {
		    public void send(String message) { }
		}
		@Service
		public class SmsNotificationService implements NotificationService {
		    public void send(String message) { }
		}
	=> Added new notification types without modifying existing logic, we can add WhatsAppNotification without modifying exsting code
				

	3. Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP)
	4. Interface Segregation Principle (ISP) :
		=> Instead of fat interfaces, we created small, focused interfaces.
			ΓÙÔ Wrong
				interface UserOperations {
				    login();
				    register();
				    generateReport();
				}

			Γ£à interface AuthService {
				    login();
				    register();
				}
				
				interface ReportService {
				    generateReport();
				}

	5. Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)

	=> In our Spring Boot project, we followed SOLID principles by separating responsibilities across controller, service, and repository layers.
		We used interfaces and dependency injection to follow Open/Closed and Dependency Inversion principles.
		Fat interfaces were avoided using Interface Segregation, and inheritance was designed carefully to respect Liskov Substitution.
		This helped us write clean, maintainable, and easily testable code


198. How ConcurrentHashMap Works (Before Java 8) and after java 8 ?

		≡Óõ╣ Java 7 and Below
				It used Segment-based locking.
					Map divided into multiple segments
					Each segment had its own lock
					Only one thread per segment could write
				≡ÓôÔ This reduced locking compared to Hashtable, but still had segment-level lock.

		≡ÓÜÇ What Changed in Java 8?
	 		1. No More Segments
					Java 8 removed Segment class completely.
				Instead, it uses:
					CAS (Compare-And-Swap)
					Synchronized block at bucket level
					Node-based locking

			2. Uses CAS for Updates
				For insertion:
					If bucket is empty ΓÁÈ use CAS (no lock)
					If bucket not empty ΓÁÈ synchronize only that bucket
				Much faster than segment locking.

			3. Tree Structure for High Collisions
					Just like HashMap in Java 8:
					If bucket size > 8 ΓÁÈ convert linked list into Red-Black Tree

			4.Better Parallelism
					Multiple threads can operate on different buckets simultaneously.
					Reads are mostly non-blocking.

		| Feature            | Java 7        | Java 8                      |
		| ------------------ | ------------- | --------------------------- |
		| Locking            | Segment-level | Bucket-level                |
		| Segments           | Yes           | ΓÙÔ Removed                   |
		| Collision Handling | LinkedList    | LinkedList + Red-Black Tree |
		| Performance        | Good          | ≡ÓÜÇ Better                   |



199. what is optmistic and pesimistic lock in hibernate ?
	≡ÓõÉ Locking in Hibernate
			Locking is used to handle concurrent updates on the same database record and maintain data consistency.
			
	
	Hibernate supports two types of locking:
		Optimistic Locking
		Pessimistic Locking
		
	1. Optimistic Locking
	
		≡Óõ╣ What it is
				Assumes conflicts are rare
				No database lock while reading
				Checks data consistency at update time
				
	Eg:	@Entity
		class Order {

			@Id
			private Long id;

			@Version
			private int version;

			private String status;
		}
		
	Flow:
		Two users read the same record (version = 1)
			User A updates ΓÁÈ version becomes 2
			User B updates ΓÁÈ ΓÙÔ OptimisticLockException

		≡Óõ╣ When to use
			Read-heavy applications
			Less chance of concurrent updates
			High performance required

		≡ÓôÔ Interview line:
			=> Optimistic locking uses a version column and checks for conflicts during update, without locking the database row.
			
	2. Pessimistic Locking
			What it is
				Assumes conflicts are common
				Locks the database row immediately
				Other transactions must wait
				
		≡Óõ╣ When to use
				High contention data
				Financial transactions
				Inventory / seat booking systems
				
			eg:	entityManager.find(
				   Order.class,
				   id,
				   LockModeType.PESSIMISTIC_WRITE
				);

			≡ÓôÔ Interview line:
				Pessimistic locking locks the database row so that no other transaction can't modify it until the lock is released.
				
				
				
	| Feature           | Optimistic Locking      | Pessimistic Locking |
	| ----------------- | ----------------------- | ------------------- |
	| Lock Type         | No DB lock              | DB row lock         |
	| Conflict Handling | At commit time          | At read time        |
	| Performance       | High                    | Lower               |
	| Waiting           | No waiting              | Other threads wait  |
	| Failure           | OptimisticLockException | Lock timeout / wait |
	
	
200. If two applications update the same data simultaneously, how do you ensure consistency?
		=> Optimistic Locking (Most Common) -> Uses a version column
		=> Pessimistic Locking (Strong Consistency) -> Locks the database row immediately
		=> Database Transaction Isolation Levels -> Use proper isolation to prevent issues like dirty or lost updates.
		=> Idempotency (API Level Protection) -> Use Idempotency Key : Same request ΓÁÈ processed only once
		=> Atomic Updates (DB Level) -> get example from chatgpt
		
201. Why does a Spring Boot app start successfully but fail after a few minutes?
		1. Database connection pool exhaustion (MOST COMMON)
			Symptoms :
				App runs for a few minutes
				Suddenly DB calls hang or fail
			Errors like:
				HikariPool-1 - Connection is not available
				
		Why it happens
			Connections are not closed
			Long-running queries
			Too many concurrent requests
				
		2. Memory leak ΓÁÈ OutOfMemoryError
			Symptoms :
				App slows down
				Suddenly crashes

				Error:
				java.lang.OutOfMemoryError: Java heap space

				Common causes :
					Storing data in static maps
					Unbounded cache
					Large objects kept in memory
					Infinite list growth

		3. Thread pool exhaustion
			Symptoms :
				App is up
					Requests stop responding
					No crash, just hangs

				Why :
					Too many async tasks
					ExecutorService not sized properly
					Blocking calls inside @Async
					
		4. External service failure (API / Kafka / Redis)
			Symptoms :
				App runs fine initially
				After some time ΓÁÈ errors
				Stack traces with timeouts

				Why :
					External service goes down
					No timeout / retry handling

				Fix :
					Add timeouts
					Circuit breaker (Resilience4j)
					Retry with backoff
					
		5. Expired credentials / tokens
			Symptoms :
				Works initially
				Fails after token expiry
				Errors like 401, 403, auth failure

				Common with :
					OAuth
					JWT
					Cloud services

				Fix :
					Refresh tokens
					DonΓÇÕt hardcode short-lived credentials
					
		6. Symptoms
			App starts
				Fails exactly after X minutes

			Example

				@Scheduled(fixedRate = 60000)
				public void job() {
				   throw new RuntimeException();
				}

			Fix :
			Add try-catch
			Log errors
			Do not crash scheduler thread
			
			
			
202. 5xx = Server-side failure
		The request:
			Γ£õ reached your API
			Γ£õ passed gateway / load balancer
			ΓÙÔ failed inside your application or infrastructure
			
			
203. what happen when multiple configuration conflict at startup ?

		=> When multiple configurations conflict at startup, Spring Boot resolves them using priority rules.
		   If the conflict cannot be resolved, the application fails to start.
		   
		=> What does ΓÇ£configuration conflictΓÇÙ mean?
				A conflict happens when:
				The same property is defined multiple times
				OR two beans/configs try to do incompatible things
				
		Case 1: Same property defined in multiple places (MOST COMMON) :
		
			What happens?
				Spring Boot does NOT fail
				≡ÓÀÊ It chooses the value with higher priority
					Priority order (high ΓÁÈ low)
						Command-line arguments
						JVM system properties
						Environment variables
						application-{profile}.yml
						application.yml / properties

				Γ£à Result: App starts using highest-priority value
				
		Case 2: Conflicting Beans (Startup FAILURE) : two beans of same types
				=> can use @Primary
						   @Qualifier
						   
		Case 3: Conflicting Auto-configurations :
			Example
				You define a DataSource
					Auto-configuration also tries to create one

				What happens?
					Spring Boot uses @ConditionalOnMissingBean
					Your bean wins
					App starts fine

				Γ£à No failure (by design)
				
		Case 4: Invalid / incompatible configuration values
				Eg : server.port=ABC
				ΓÙÔ Startup fails immediately
				
		Case 5: Profile conflicts
			eg1 : spring.profiles.active=dev,prod -> it will fail	
			eg2 : server.port: 8080
				  server.port: 9090
						
				What happens?
					Last profile wins
					Can cause unexpected behavior
					App usually starts, but wrongly configured
					
		| Conflict Type         | Result                  |
		| --------------------- | ----------------------- |
		| Same property         | Higher priority wins    |
		| Duplicate beans       | ΓÙÔ Startup fails         |
		| Auto-config vs custom | Custom wins             |
		| Invalid value         | ΓÙÔ Startup fails         |
		| Profile conflict      | Last profile wins       |
		| Dependency conflict   | ΓÙÔ Fail or runtime crash |
		
		
204. How can a small change in properties cause a major outage?
		1. Reducing thread pool by mistake (VERY COMMON) -> 
			eg : server.tomcat.max-threads=50   # was 200
				What happens
					Each request needs a thread
					Under normal load ΓÁÈ fine
					Under peak load ΓÁÈ no threads
					Requests queue ΓÁÈ timeout ΓÁÈ 503 / 5xx
					
		2. Database connection pool misconfiguration
			Eg: small chgange -> spring.datasource.hikari.maximum-pool-size=5   # was 30
				What happens :
					Only 5 DB connections available
					Requests wait for connection
					Timeout occurs
					Thread pool also gets blocked

					Result
					≡ÓõÑ Cascading failure
					≡ÓõÑ All APIs return 5xx
					
		3. Timeout values too aggressive
			Eg : spring.datasource.hikari.connection-timeout=1000
					What happens
						DB needs 1.5s under load
						App times out early
						Retries multiply traffic

						Result
						ΓÙÔ Retry storm
						ΓÙÔ DB overload
						ΓÙÔ Full outage
						
		4. Cache TTL change ΓÁÈ DB meltdown
			Eg: cache.ttl=10s   # was 10m
				What happens
					Cache expires frequently
					All requests hit DB
					DB cannot handle traffic

					Result
					≡ÓõÑ DB exhaustion
					≡ÓõÑ API failures everywhere
					
		5. Disabling circuit breaker accidentally
			Eg : resilience4j.circuitbreaker.enabled=false
				What happens
					Downstream service slows
					All requests wait
					Threads blocked

					Result
					ΓÙÔ One bad service takes down entire system
					
		6. Profile change (silent killer)
			Eg : spring.profiles.active=prod instead of spring.profiles.active=staging
				What happens
					Wrong DB
					Wrong credentials
					Wrong limits
					Wrong URLs

					Result
					≡ÓõÑ Immediate outage
					≡ÓõÑ Data inconsistency risk
					
		7. Logging level change
			Eg : logging.level.root=DEBUG
				What happens
					Heavy logging
					Disk I/O spikes
					CPU increases
					App slows ΓÁÈ timeouts

					Result
					ΓÙÔ Performance collapse under load
					
					
205. How to Detect Blocking Calls in a Spring Boot App ?

		1. Check Thread Dumps (MOST PRACTICAL WAY) :
		
			 Take a thread dump when the app is slow:
				jstack <PID> > threaddump.txt

				Then search for:
					WAITING
					BLOCKED
					TIMED_WAITING

				If you see many threads like:
						"http-nio-8080-exec-45" waiting on socketRead
						"http-nio-8080-exec-46" waiting on JDBC

						≡ÓÀÊ That means threads are blocked.
						
		2. Use Spring Boot Actuator (Quick Detection)
			Enable:
				management.endpoints.web.exposure.include=threaddump,metrics
				
				Check:
					/actuator/threaddump

				Look for:
					Many threads in WAITING
					Long-running threads
					
		3. Look for Common Blocking Code Patterns :
		
			ΓÙÔ Blocking REST call
				restTemplate.getForObject(...)
			ΓÙÔ Blocking DB call
				repository.findAll()
			ΓÙÔ Thread sleep
				Thread.sleep(5000);
			ΓÙÔ Synchronized block
				synchronized(this) {
				   // heavy logic
				}
				
		4. Use Monitoring Tools (Production Level) :
				JVisualVM
				JConsole
				YourKit
				New Relic
				Datadog

				Look for:
					High thread count
					High response time
					CPU idle but app slow (means waiting/blocking)
					
206. What is Graceful Shutdown?
		=> The application stops accepting new requests, finishes in-flight work safely, 
		   releases resources cleanly, and exits without data loss.
		   
		=> What happens during a graceful shutdown (sequence)
				1. Stop taking new requests
				2. Allow in-flight requests to complete
				3. Finish background jobs / async tasks
				4. Commit or rollback transactions
				5. Close DB connections, threads, resources
				6. Exit process cleanly
				
	=> How do you ensure graceful shutdown without data loss (Spring Boot)
			1. How do you ensure graceful shutdown without data loss (Spring Boot)
				Eg : server.shutdown=graceful
					 spring.lifecycle.timeout-per-shutdown-phase=30s
					 
					What this does :
						Stops accepting new HTTP requests
						Waits up to 30 seconds for active requests to finish

			2. Configure the web server to wait for requests
				For Tomcat
					server.tomcat.accept-count=100
					server.tomcat.max-threads=200

					During shutdown:
						New connections rejected
						Existing requests allowed to complete
						
			3. Gracefully stop scheduled jobs
				Eg : @Scheduled(fixedRate = 60000)
						public void job() {
							if (shuttingDown.get()) return;
							// job logic
						}
						'
			4. Ensure DB consistency (VERY IMPORTANT)

					Use transactions :
 
						Let in-flight transactions complete
						Close connections cleanly

						@Transactional
						public void processOrder() {
						   // atomic operation
						}

						Spring waits for active transactions before shutdown.
						
		Quick checklist (save this ≡Óºá)
			Γ£à server.shutdown=graceful
			Γ£à Shutdown timeout configured
			Γ£à Async executor waits
			Γ£à Transactions used
			Γ£à Schedulers handled
			Γ£à Message consumers commit
			Γ£à Readiness probe enabled
			

207. how do you duebug issue that appear only on production ?
		=> Issues that appear only in production are usually due to scale, data, timing, or environment differences.
		
		=> Quick checklist (save this ≡Óºá)
			Γ£à Logs first
			Γ£à Metrics next
			Γ£à Thread dump if slow
			Γ£à Compare configs
			Γ£à Check schedulers & dependencies
			Γ£à Stabilize ΓÁÈ then fix
			
	=> Interview-ready answer Γ¡É
			To debug issues that appear only in production, I rely on observability rather than reproduction. 
			I analyze logs, metrics, and thread dumps to understand resource usage, timing patterns, and failures,
			compare production and non-production configurations, and identify bottlenecks like thread starvation,
			connection pool exhaustion, blocking calls, or external dependency failures.

208. What are Virtual Threads (Project Loom, Java 21) and how do they improve backend scalability?

	What are Virtual Threads?
		Virtual Threads are lightweight threads introduced in Java (Project Loom) that dramatically reduce the cost of creating and managing threads.
		Introduced as preview in Java 19
		Stabilized in Java 21
		Implemented by the JVM (not the OS)
		
		≡ÓÀÊ In simple words:
			Virtual threads allow you to run millions of concurrent tasks using the same blocking-style code.

	Platform Thread vs Virtual Thread (Simple Comparison)
		| Feature                | Platform Thread  | Virtual Thread            |
		| ---------------------- | ---------------- | ------------------------- |
		| Backed by OS thread    | Yes              | ΓÙÔ No                      |
		| Creation cost          | Expensive        | Very cheap                |
		| Memory per thread      | ~1ΓÇô2 MB          | Few KB                    |
		| Max concurrent threads | Thousands        | Millions                  |
		| Blocking call impact   | Blocks OS thread | ΓÙÔ DoesnΓÇÕt block OS thread |

	=> In traditional systems, each request blocks an OS thread while waiting for DB or external APIs. 
	   Virtual threads are lightweight and when they wait, they release the underlying OS thread. 
	   This allows the system to handle thousands of concurrent I/O requests using simple blocking code without thread exhaustion

209. what is WebFlux and how does it helps ?
	=> WebFlux exists to solve one big problem:
			How to handle a very large number of concurrent requests using fewer threads.

	=> Before WebFlux:
			Spring MVC = one thread per request
			Threads get blocked on DB / API calls
			Under heavy load ΓÁÈ thread exhaustion ΓÁÈ 5xx

	=> with webflux -> A reactive, non-blocking web framework
			Uses non-blocking I/O
			Never waits
			When DB/API responds, a signal is sent
			Result handled via callback

210. What is Thread Starvation ?
	=> Requests are waiting to be processed, but no threads are available to execute them.
			Not because CPU is slow ΓÇõ
			but because all threads are busy or blocked

211. How do CompletableFuture and Structured Concurrency enhance async REST handling ?
	=> ≡Óº╡ First: The Problem in Async REST
				In your Fraud Service, suppose for one claim you must:
					Call Policy Service
					Call Customer Service
					Call GAF (external)
					Fetch DB history

					If you do it sequentially:
						Call A (500ms)
						Call B (500ms)
						Call C (500ms)
							Total = 1500ms
				=> But these calls are independent.
				
				We want:
					Call A, B, C together
					Total ΓÊê 500ms
					
	1. CompletableFuture ΓÇô Improves Parallel REST Calls
		What It Does :
			CompletableFuture allows:
				Running tasks asynchronously
				Combining results
				Handling errors
				Executing in parallel

		≡Óºá Simple Example (Your Fraud Case) :  all below are run asyncrnously or parallely and at end they combine and process

		eg:	CompletableFuture<Policy> policyFuture =
			    CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> policyClient.getPolicy());
			
			CompletableFuture<Customer> customerFuture =
			    CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> customerClient.getCustomer());
			
			CompletableFuture<FraudScore> gafFuture =
			    CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> gafClient.getScore());
			
			CompletableFuture.allOf(policyFuture, customerFuture, gafFuture).join();
			
			Policy policy = policyFuture.join();
			Customer customer = customerFuture.join();
			FraudScore score = gafFuture.join();

	How It Enhances Async REST Handling :
		Γ£õ Reduces overall latency
		Γ£õ Improves response time
		Γ£õ Uses thread pool efficiently
		Γ£õ Allows combining multiple REST responses

	≡ÓÜ¿ Problem With CompletableFuture
			When logic becomes complex:
				Hard to manage cancellation
				Hard to handle partial failure
				If one fails ΓÁÈ others still run
				Thread leaks possible
			It becomes messy in large systems.

so, in Java 21 inriduced called Structured Concurrency ΓÇô Cleaner & Safer Async (project Loom)

	≡Óºá Idea Behind Structured Concurrency
			If a parent request fails:
				All child tasks should stop
			
			If one child fails:
				Cancel others
				Everything is managed in one block.

	eg:		try (var scope = new StructuredTaskScope.ShutdownOnFailure()) {
			
			    Future<Policy> policy = scope.fork(() -> policyClient.getPolicy());
			    Future<Customer> customer = scope.fork(() -> customerClient.getCustomer());
			    Future<FraudScore> gaf = scope.fork(() -> gafClient.getScore());
			
			    scope.join();           // Wait for all
			    scope.throwIfFailed();  // If any failed
			
			    return combine(policy.resultNow(),
			                   customer.resultNow(),
			                   gaf.resultNow());
			}


		Γ£à Why This Is Better
		
		Γ£õ Automatic cancellation
		Γ£õ Cleaner error handling
		Γ£õ No thread leaks
		Γ£õ Easier debugging
		Γ£õ Safer parallel execution

	| Feature            | CompletableFuture | Structured Concurrency |
	| ------------------ | ----------------- | ---------------------- |
	| Parallel execution | Γ£à                 | Γ£à                      |
	| Error handling     | Complex           | Clean                  |
	| Auto cancellation  | ΓÙÔ Manual          | Γ£à Automatic            |
	| Readability        | Medium            | High                   |
	| Debugging          | Hard              | Easy                   |

	CompletableFuture:
		ΓÇ£Let me run tasks in parallel.ΓÇÙ
	
	Structured Concurrency:
		ΓÇ£Let me run tasks in parallel safely and manage them properly.ΓÇÙ

212. Key Features Introduced in Java 8 :
	1. Lambda Expressions -> Anonymous functions that reduce boilerplate code.
	2. Functional Interfaces -> An interface with only one abstract method.
	3. Stream API -> Process collections in a functio nal and declarative way.
	4. Optional Class -> Avoid NullPointerException.
	5. Default & Static Methods in Interfaces -> Interfaces can have method implementations.
	6. Method References -> Shortcut for lambdas.
	7. New Date & Time API (java.time)  eg : LocalDate today = LocalDate.now();
											 LocalDateTime now = LocalDateTime.now();
	8. Parallel Streams -> Process streams using multiple CPU cores.
	9. Nashorn JavaScript Engine -> Execute JavaScript inside Java.
		eg : ScriptEngine engine = new ScriptEngineManager().getEngineByName("nashorn");

213. How to configure and use two different databases in a single Spring Boot application ?
	Each DB has:
		Its own DataSource
		Its own EntityManager
		Its own TransactionManager
		Its own Repository package

	Eg :@Configuration
		@EnableJpaRepositories(
		    basePackages = "com.app.claim.repo",
		    entityManagerFactoryRef = "primaryEntityManager",
		    transactionManagerRef = "primaryTransactionManager"
		)
		public class PrimaryDbConfig {
		
		    @Bean
		    @Primary
		    @ConfigurationProperties("spring.datasource.primary")
		    public DataSource primaryDataSource() {
		        return DataSourceBuilder.create().build();
		    }
		
		    @Bean
		    @Primary
		    public LocalContainerEntityManagerFactoryBean primaryEntityManager(
		            EntityManagerFactoryBuilder builder) {
		        return builder
		                .dataSource(primaryDataSource())
		                .packages("com.app.claim.entity")
		                .persistenceUnit("primaryDB")
		                .build();
		    }
		
		    @Bean
		    @Primary
		    public PlatformTransactionManager primaryTransactionManager(
		            @Qualifier("primaryEntityManager") EntityManagerFactory emf) {
		        return new JpaTransactionManager(emf);
		    }
		}

	=>	for 2nd Database
			@Configuration
			@EnableJpaRepositories(
			    basePackages = "com.app.fraud.repo",
			    entityManagerFactoryRef = "secondaryEntityManager",
			    transactionManagerRef = "secondaryTransactionManager"
			)
			public class SecondaryDbConfig { .....  same as above but diff config

=>  To use two databases in Spring Boot, we configure separate DataSources, EntityManagers, and TransactionManagers for each database. 
    Each set of entities and repositories is mapped to its own persistence unit using @EnableJpaRepositories. One datasource is marked as primary, 
	and transactions are handled independently per database to avoid cross-database consistency issues.ΓÇÙ

214. Explain Caching in Spring Boot implementation and where it fits best ?
	=> Caching stores frequently used data in fast memory (RAM) so the application doesnΓÇÕt hit the database or external services every time.
	=> Goal: Reduce latency + reduce load on DB/external APIs.

	=> Best Candidates for Caching
			Read-heavy APIs
			Rarely changing data
			Expensive operations

	=> Enabling Caching in Spring Boot
		@EnableCaching
		@SpringBootApplication
		public class Application {
		}

	=> Key Cache Annotations (Core Concept)
		| Annotation    | Purpose             |
		| ------------- | ------------------- |
		| `@Cacheable`  | Read & store result |
		| `@CachePut`   | Update cache        |
		| `@CacheEvict` | Remove cache        |

	Eg: @Cacheable(value = "policyCache", key = "#policyId")
		public Policy getPolicy(String policyId) {
		    return policyRepository.findById(policyId).orElseThrow();
		}

=> Cache Update : everytime we call updatePolicy it will update db and same update cache aswell 
		@CachePut(value = "policyCache", key = "#policy.id")
		public Policy updatePolicy(Policy policy) {
		    return policyRepository.save(policy);
		}

=> Cache Evict : evrytime we call deletePolicy It will delete record from db and same removed data from cache
		@CacheEvict(value = "policyCache", key = "#policyId")
		public void deletePolicy(String policyId) {
		    policyRepository.deleteById(policyId);
		}


215. How you handle deployment tasks ?
	1. Before deployment, I ensure that my code is production-ready by verifying environment-specific configurations such as application.yml profiles, 
	   database connections, Kafka topics, cache settings, and third-party API endpoints.

	2. I make sure database changes are backward-compatible and coordinate schema changes carefully. I also validate that feature flags, timeouts, circuit 					breakers, and cache TTLs are correctly configured for the target environment.

	3. During deployment, I support the DevOps team by verifying the build artifacts, Docker images, and application startup logs. After deployment, I perform 			   smoke testing on critical APIs, monitor logs and metrics, and quickly address issues like 5xx errors, latency spikes, or thread pool exhaustion.

	4. In case of issues, I help in rollback or hotfix deployments and participate in root cause analysis to prevent recurrence



216. How do you implement ProducerΓÇôConsumer in Java ?
	=> I use BlockingQueue for ProducerΓÇôConsumer because it handles synchronization and blocking automatically.

			≡ÓõÀ WhatΓÇÕs happening internally?
				put() ΓÁÈ waits if buffer is full
				take() ΓÁÈ waits if buffer is empty
			
			JVM handles thread coordination
				≡ÓÀÊ No race condition
				≡ÓÀÊ No busy waiting

217. Explain difference between ExecutorService, ForkJoinPool and CompletableFuture ?
		=> ExecutorService manages thread pools and executes asynchronous tasks. 
		=> ForkJoinPool is specialized for recursive divide-and-conquer CPU-intensive tasks using work-stealing. 			   
		=> CompletableFuture is a higher-level abstraction that enables chaining, combining, and handling asynchronous tasks more elegantly, especially useful 				for parallel REST calls.

218. Difference between @Controller, @RestController and @ControllerAdvice.
	1.  @Controller :
			=> It is used in Spring MVC applications where you return views (HTML/JSP/Thymeleaf).
			=> Used for UI-based applications

	2. @RestController :
			=> @RestController is used for REST APIs.
			=> @RestController = @Controller + @ResponseBody -> Always returns data (JSON/XML), not views

	3. @ControllerAdvice :
			=> @ControllerAdvice is used for global exception handling and cross-cutting concerns across controllers.

219. Explain lazy loading vs eager loading ΓÇõ how to avoid LazyInitializationException.
		=> What is Lazy Loading :
			=>  Data is loaded only when you actually access it.
			eg : Example
					@Entity
					class Order {
					    @OneToMany(fetch = FetchType.LAZY)
					    private List<Item> items;
					}
					
					Order is fetched first
					items are NOT loaded immediately
					DB call happens only when order.getItems() is called

		=> What is Eager Loading :	
				=> Related data is loaded immediately along with the parent.
				eg:	Example
					@OneToMany(fetch = FetchType.EAGER)
					private List<Item> items;

						Order + Items fetched in one go
						DB hit happens immediately

		=> What is LazyInitializationException? eg : failed to lazily initialize a collection of role no session or entity manager

			Why it happens?
				Because:
					Entity is fetched
					Hibernate session is closed
					Lazy data is accessed outside transaction

		=> Order order = orderRepository.findById(id);
		   order.getItems(); // ΓÙÔ LazyInitializationException

		Solution  : How to Avoid LazyInitializationException
		1. Access Lazy Data Inside Transaction
			eg :    @Transactional
					public Order getOrder(Long id) {
					    Order order = orderRepository.findById(id).orElseThrow();
					    order.getItems().size(); // initializes lazy collection
					    return order;
					}
		2. use joins
		3. DTO Projection
		4. Open Session In View (OSIV) (NOT RECOMMENDED) eg:spring.jpa.open-in-view: true
		5. Change FetchType to EAGER (BAD PRACTICE) eg : @OneToMany(fetch = FetchType.EAGER)

221. Implement a REST endpoint to fetch the last 10 activities of a user.
		Controller :
				@GetMapping("/{userId}/activities")
			    public List<Activity> getLastActivities(@PathVariable String userId) {
			        return service.getLast10Activities(userId);
			    }
		Service :
				public List<Activity> getLast10Activities(String userId) {
			        return repository.findTop10ByUserIdOrderByCreatedAtDesc(userId);
			    }
		Repository :
				public interface ActivityRepository extends JpaRepository<Activity, Long> {
				    @Query(
				        value = """
				            SELECT *
				            FROM user_activity
				            WHERE user_id = :userId
				            ORDER BY created_at DESC
				            LIMIT 10
				        """,
				        nativeQuery = true
				    )
				    List<Activity> findLast10Activities(@Param("userId") String userId);
				}

224. What is CopyOnWriteArrayList ?
	=> CopyOnWriteArrayList creates a fresh copy of the underlying array every time a write operation (add/remove/update) occurs, while readers continue to read 		from the old, unmodified array.

	=> CopyOnWriteArrayList is a thread-safe List implementation in Java designed for concurrent read-heavy scenarios.

	Eg : CopyOnWriteArrayList<String> list = new CopyOnWriteArrayList<>();
			list.add("A");
			list.add("B");
			
			for (String s : list) {
			    list.add("C");   // No ConcurrentModificationException, but in plain ArrayList will get Exception
			    System.out.println(s);
			}
			System.out.println(list); 

		Output:
			A
			B
			[A, B, C, C] 

225. What are the differences between Synchronized Collection and Concurrent Collection in Java?

	1.  => Synchronized Collection is thread-safe but may have performance issues, 
		=> while Concurrent Collection is optimized for concurrent access.

	2. => Synchronized Collection uses synchronized keyword to achieve thread-safety, 
	   => while Concurrent Collection uses non-blocking algorithms like CAS (Compare and Swap)

	3. => Synchronized Collection locks the entire collection during modification, leading to potential performance issue	
	   => while Concurrent Collection allows multiple threads to access and modify the collection concurrently

	4. => Examples of Synchronized Collection include synchronizedList, synchronizedSet, synchronizedMap, 
	   => while examples of Concurrent Collection include ConcurrentHashMap, CopyOnWriteArrayList, CopyOnWriteArraySet

226. How to sort emp on emp salary without using streams
		 List<Employee> list = Arrays.asList(
            new Employee("manju", 5000, "male", "3", "IT"),
            new Employee("Teju", 5000, "female", "2", "DEV"),
            new Employee("Ram", 2000, "male", "7", "QA"),
            new Employee("Ram", 7000, "male", "7", "QA"),
            new Employee("Ram", 9000, "male", "7", "QA")
		    );
		
		    Collections.sort(list, new Comparator<Employee>(){
		        @Override
		        public int compare(Employee e1, Employee e2){
		//            return e2.getSalary() - e1.getSalary();  //descending order on salary
		            return e1.getSalary() - e2.getSalary();  //asceding order on salary
					
					 // for string comparision 
					 return e2.getName().compareTo(e1.getName()); // descending order 
		        }
		    });
		    
		    list.forEach(n-> System.out.println(n.getSalary()));

228. diff between gradle and maven and why springboot prefer Maven ?
	=> Maven is a convention-based build tool using XML with a fixed lifecycle
	=> while Gradle is a flexible and faster build tool using Groovy or Kotlin DSL.
	=> Gradle supports incremental builds and caching, making it more suitable for large, modern microservices projects

	=> Spring Boot defaults to Maven for simplicity, standardization, and mature dependency management

229. How to configure CORS and JWT Filter
	CORS :
		@Bean
		public WebMvcConfigurer corsConfigurer() {
		    return new WebMvcConfigurer() {
		        public void addCorsMappings(CorsRegistry registry) {
		            registry.addMapping("/**")
		                    .allowedOrigins("http://localhost:3000")
		                    .allowedMethods("*")
		                    .allowedHeaders("*");
		        }
		    };
		}

	JWT Filter chain :
		@Bean
		public SecurityFilterChain filterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
		
		    http.csrf().disable()
		        .authorizeHttpRequests(auth -> auth
		            .requestMatchers("/auth/**").permitAll()
		            .anyRequest().authenticated()
		        )
		        .addFilterBefore(jwtFilter,
		                UsernamePasswordAuthenticationFilter.class);
		
		    return http.build();
		}

230. Two-Phase Commit (2PC) ?
		=> Two-Phase Commit (2PC) is a distributed transaction protocol that ensures all participating systems either commit a transaction together or roll it 			   back together ΓÇõ guaranteeing atomicity across multiple databases or services.

	ΓÜÕ∩╕Â How Two-Phase Commit Works (Step-by-Step)
	≡ÓÓí Phase 1: Prepare (Voting Phase)
		Coordinator asks all participants: ΓÇ£Can you commit?ΓÇÙ
		Each participant:
			Performs checks
			Locks resources
			Replies YES (ready) or NO (cannot commit)
	
	≡ÓÓó Phase 2: Commit / Rollback Phase
		If ALL say YES ΓÁÈ Coordinator sends COMMIT
		If ANY say NO ΓÁÈ Coordinator sends ROLLBACK
		Participants act accordingly and release locks.

	1. Singleton -> only one instance of bean per spring container (default)

	3. Request	-> single bean instance for HTTP request
	4. session -> single bean instace for http session
	5. global session - a single bean instance for global http session.

	Note : bean cretaed for prototype -> spring will not remove the old object from memory we have to remove manually same as below
		Prototype bean holds:
			=> prototype bean -> can manually destory using cleanup(), assiging it null, destory(), clean 
			=> DB connection
			=> File handle
			=> Thread
			=> Long-running background objects

	but for all scopes spring will destory the bean eg: session, request, global session
